<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Termination of a taxon by the death of the last member</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Extinct" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Extinct_(disambiguation)" title="Extinct (disambiguation)">Extinct (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Extinction_(disambiguation)" title="Extinction (disambiguation)">Extinction (disambiguation)</a>. For lists, see <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_extinct_species" title="Lists of extinct species">Lists of extinct species</a>.</div>

<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="915" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/16px-Tree_of_life.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/24px-Tree_of_life.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tree_of_life.svg/32px-Tree_of_life.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Evolutionary_biology" title="Portal:Evolutionary biology">Evolutionary biology portal</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Evolutionary_biology" title="Category:Evolutionary biology">Category</a></span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_evolutionary_biology_articles" title="Index of evolutionary biology articles">Related topics</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Evolutionary_biology" title="Template:Evolutionary biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Evolutionary_biology" title="Template talk:Evolutionary biology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Evolutionary_biology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Extinction</b> is the termination of a kind of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> or of a group of kinds (<a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a>), usually a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. The moment of extinction is generally considered to be the <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Endling" title="Endling">last individual</a> of the species, although the <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">capacity to breed and recover</a> may have been lost before this point. Because a species' potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">range</a> may be very large, determining this moment is difficult, and is usually done retrospectively. This difficulty leads to phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_taxon" title="Lazarus taxon">Lazarus taxa</a>, where a species presumed extinct abruptly "reappears" (typically in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil record</a>) after a period of apparent absence.
</p><p>More than 99% of all species that ever <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">lived</a> on Earth, amounting to over five billion species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> are estimated to have died out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jablonski2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jablonski2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-5">[5]</a></sup> It is estimated that there are currently around 8.7 million species of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a> globally,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLoSbiologyspeciescensus_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLoSbiologyspeciescensus-6">[6]</a></sup> and possibly many times more if <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, like bacteria, are included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-7">[7]</a></sup> Notable extinct animal species include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-avian_dinosaurs" title="Non-avian dinosaurs">non-avian dinosaurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saber-toothed_cats" title="Saber-toothed cats">saber-toothed cats</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dodos" title="Dodos">dodos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammoths" title="Mammoths">mammoths</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_sloths" title="Ground sloths">ground sloths</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylacines" title="Thylacines">thylacines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trilobites" title="Trilobites">trilobites</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_toads" title="Golden toads">golden toads</a>.
</p><p>Through <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, species arise through the process of <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>—where new varieties of organisms arise and thrive when they are able to find and exploit an <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a>—and species become extinct when they are no longer able to survive in changing conditions or against superior <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">competition</a>. The relationship between animals and their ecological niches has been firmly established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates-8">[8]</a></sup> A typical species becomes extinct within 10 million years of its first appearance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-5">[5]</a></sup> although some species, called <a href="/wiki/Living_fossil" title="Living fossil">living fossils</a>, survive with little to no <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> change for hundreds of millions of years.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">Mass extinctions</a> are relatively rare events; however, isolated extinctions are quite common. Only recently have extinctions been recorded and scientists have become alarmed at the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction#Contemporary_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">current high rate of extinctions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Most species that become extinct are never scientifically documented. Some scientists estimate that up to half of presently existing plant and animal species may become extinct by 2100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-14">[14]</a></sup> A 2018 report indicated that the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic diversity</a> of 300 mammalian species erased during the human era since the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> would require 5 to 7 million years to recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis2018_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis2018-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the 2019 <i><a href="/wiki/Global_Assessment_Report_on_Biodiversity_and_Ecosystem_Services" title="Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services">Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPBES" title="IPBES">IPBES</a>, the biomass of wild mammals has fallen by 82%, natural ecosystems have lost about half their area and a million species are at risk of extinction—all largely as a result of human actions. Twenty-five percent of plant and animal species are <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">threatened</a> with extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190506_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190506-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPBES-20190506_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPBES-20190506-18">[18]</a></sup> In a subsequent report, IPBES listed unsustainable fishing, hunting and logging as being some of the primary drivers of the global extinction crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2019, one million species of plants and animals were at risk of extinction. At least 571 species have been lost since 1750, but likely many more. The main cause of the extinctions is the destruction of natural habitats by human activities, such as cutting down forests and converting land into fields for farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Dagger_(mark)" title="Dagger (mark)">dagger symbol</a> (†) placed next to the name of a species or other taxon normally indicates its status as extinct.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Examples of species and subspecies that are extinct include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">Dodo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_paddlefish" title="Chinese paddlefish">Chinese paddlefish</a> (last seen in 2003; declared extinct in 2022)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Great_auk" title="Great auk">Great auk</a> (last confirmed pair was killed in the 1840s)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">Thylacine</a> (last known member died in captivity in 1936; unconfirmed sightings continued after this date)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kaua%CA%BBi_%CA%BB%C5%8D%CA%BB%C5%8D" title="Kauaʻi ʻōʻō">Kauai O'o</a> (last known member was heard in 1987; the entire <a href="/wiki/Mohoidae" title="Mohoidae">Mohoidae</a> became extinct with it)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spectacled_cormorant" title="Spectacled cormorant">Spectacled cormorant</a> (last known members were said to live in the 1850s)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carolina_parakeet" title="Carolina parakeet">Carolina parakeet</a> (last known member named <a href="/wiki/Incas_(Carolina_parakeet)" title="Incas (Carolina parakeet)">Incas</a> died in captivity in 1918; declared extinct in 1939)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Passenger_pigeon" title="Passenger pigeon">Passenger pigeon</a> (last known member named <a href="/wiki/Martha_(passenger_pigeon)" title="Martha (passenger pigeon)">Martha</a> died in captivity in 1914)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tasmanian_emu" title="Tasmanian emu">Tasmanian emu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kangaroo_Island_emu" title="Kangaroo Island emu">Kangaroo Island emu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/King_Island_emu" title="King Island emu">King island emu</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LepidodendronOhio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1251" data-file-width="1821" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/220px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/330px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/LepidodendronOhio.jpg/440px-LepidodendronOhio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LepidodendronOhio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>External mold of the extinct <i><a href="/wiki/Lepidodendron" title="Lepidodendron">Lepidodendron</a></i> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Carboniferous" title="Upper Carboniferous">Upper Carboniferous</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A species is extinct when the last existing member dies. Extinction therefore becomes a certainty when there are no surviving individuals that can reproduce and create a new generation. A species may become <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">functionally extinct</a> when only a handful of individuals survive, which cannot reproduce due to poor health, age, sparse distribution over a large range, a lack of individuals of both sexes (in <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexually reproducing</a> species), or other reasons.
</p><p>Pinpointing the extinction (or <a href="/wiki/Pseudoextinction" title="Pseudoextinction">pseudoextinction</a>) of a species requires a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_species" title="Theory of species">clear definition of that species</a>. If it is to be declared extinct, the species in question must be uniquely distinguishable from any ancestor or daughter species, and from any other closely related species. Extinction of a species (or replacement by a daughter species) plays a key role in the <a href="/wiki/Punctuated_equilibrium" title="Punctuated equilibrium">punctuated equilibrium</a> hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">Stephen Jay Gould</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niles_Eldredge" title="Niles Eldredge">Niles Eldredge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Various_dinosaurs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="920" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Various_dinosaurs.png/220px-Various_dinosaurs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Various_dinosaurs.png/330px-Various_dinosaurs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Various_dinosaurs.png/440px-Various_dinosaurs.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Various_dinosaurs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of various extinct <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>; some other dinosaur lineages still flourish in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, <i>extinction</i> is sometimes used informally to refer to <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">local extinction</a>, in which a species ceases to exist in the chosen area of study, despite still existing elsewhere. Local extinctions may be made good by the reintroduction of individuals of that species taken from other locations; <a href="/wiki/Wolf_reintroduction" title="Wolf reintroduction">wolf reintroduction</a> is an example of this. Species that are not globally extinct are termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a>. Those species that are extant, yet are threatened with extinction, are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Threatened" title="Threatened">threatened</a> or <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1605" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/220px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/330px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg/440px-Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edwards%27_Dodo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, shown here in a 1626 illustration by <a href="/wiki/Roelant_Savery" title="Roelant Savery">Roelant Savery</a>, is an often-cited example of <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">modern extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Currently, an important aspect of extinction is human attempts to preserve critically endangered species. These are reflected by the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_status" title="Conservation status">conservation status</a> <a href="/wiki/Extinct_in_the_wild" title="Extinct in the wild">"extinct in the wild" (EW)</a>. Species listed under this status by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) are not known to have any living specimens in the wild and are maintained only in <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoos</a> or other artificial environments. Some of these species are functionally extinct, as they are no longer part of their natural habitat and it is unlikely the species will ever be restored to the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maas_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maas-25">[25]</a></sup> When possible, modern <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoological</a> institutions try to maintain a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viable_population" title="Viable population">viable population</a> for species preservation and possible future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reintroduction" title="Reintroduction">reintroduction</a> to the wild, through use of carefully planned <a href="/wiki/Breeding_program" title="Breeding program">breeding programs</a>.
</p><p>The extinction of one species' wild population can have knock-on effects, causing further extinctions. These are also called "chains of extinction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> This is especially common with extinction of <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a>.
</p><p>A 2018 study indicated that the sixth mass extinction started in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> could take up to 5 to 7 million years to restore 2.5 billion years of unique mammal diversity to what it was before the human era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis2018_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis2018-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pseudoextinction">Pseudoextinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pseudoextinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pseudoextinction" title="Pseudoextinction">Pseudoextinction</a></div>
<p>Extinction of a parent species where daughter species or subspecies are still extant is called pseudoextinction or phyletic extinction. Effectively, the old taxon vanishes, transformed (<a href="/wiki/Anagenesis" title="Anagenesis">anagenesis</a>) into a successor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> or split into more than one (<a href="/wiki/Cladogenesis" title="Cladogenesis">cladogenesis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pseudoextinction is difficult to demonstrate unless one has a strong chain of evidence linking a living species to members of a pre-existing species. For example, it is sometimes claimed that the extinct <i><a href="/wiki/Hyracotherium" title="Hyracotherium">Hyracotherium</a></i>, which was an early horse that shares a common ancestor with the modern <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a>, is pseudoextinct, rather than extinct, because there are several extant species of <i><a href="/wiki/Equus_(genus)" title="Equus (genus)">Equus</a></i>, including <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a>; however, as fossil species typically leave no genetic material behind, one cannot say whether <i>Hyracotherium</i> <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_horse" title="Evolution of the horse">evolved into more modern horse species</a> or merely evolved from a common ancestor with modern horses. Pseudoextinction is much easier to demonstrate for larger taxonomic groups.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lazarus_taxa">Lazarus taxa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lazarus taxa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lazarus_taxa" title="Lazarus taxa">Lazarus taxa</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Coelacanth" title="Coelacanth">coelacanth</a>, a fish related to <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a>, was considered to have been extinct since the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretaceous_Period" title="Cretaceous Period">Cretaceous Period</a>. In 1938, however, a living specimen was found off the <a href="/wiki/Chalumna_River" title="Chalumna River">Chalumna River</a> (now Tyolomnqa) on the east coast of South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinofish_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-dinofish-30">[30]</a></sup> Museum curator <a href="/wiki/Marjorie_Courtenay-Latimer" title="Marjorie Courtenay-Latimer">Marjorie Courtenay-Latimer</a> discovered the fish among the catch of a local trawler operated by Captain Hendrick Goosen, on December 23, 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinofish_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-dinofish-30">[30]</a></sup> A local chemistry professor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Leonard_Brierley_Smith" title="James Leonard Brierley Smith">JLB Smith</a>, confirmed the fish's importance with a famous cable: "MOST IMPORTANT PRESERVE SKELETON AND GILLS = FISH DESCRIBED".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dinofish_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-dinofish-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Far more recent possible or presumed extinctions of species which may turn out still to exist include the <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">thylacine</a>, or Tasmanian tiger (<i>Thylacinus cynocephalus</i>), the last known example of which died in Hobart Zoo in Tasmania in 1936; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honsh%C5%AB_wolf" title="Honshū wolf">Japanese wolf</a> (<i>Canis lupus hodophilax</i>), last sighted over 100 years ago; the <a href="/wiki/Ivory-billed_woodpecker" title="Ivory-billed woodpecker">American ivory-billed woodpecker</a> (<i>Campephilus principalis</i>), with the last universally accepted sighting in 1944; and the <a href="/wiki/Slender-billed_curlew" title="Slender-billed curlew">slender-billed curlew</a> (<i>Numenius tenuirostris</i>), not seen since 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg/170px-Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg/255px-Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg/340px-Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ectopistes_migratoriusMCN2P28CA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Passenger_pigeon" title="Passenger pigeon">passenger pigeon</a>, one of the hundreds of species of extinct birds, was hunted to extinction over the course of a few decades.</div></div></div>
<p>As long as species have been evolving, species have been going extinct. It is estimated that over 99.9% of all species that ever lived are extinct. The average lifespan of a species is 1–10 million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> although this varies widely between taxa.
There are a variety of causes that can contribute directly or indirectly to the extinction of a species or group of species. "Just as each species is unique", write Beverly and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_C._Stearns" title="Stephen C. Stearns">Stephen C. Stearns</a>, "so is each extinction ... the causes for each are varied—some subtle and complex, others obvious and simple".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stearns_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stearns-33">[33]</a></sup> Most simply, any species that cannot <a href="/wiki/Survival_skills" title="Survival skills">survive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduce</a> in its environment and cannot move to a new environment where it can do so, dies out and becomes extinct. Extinction of a species may come suddenly when an otherwise healthy species is wiped out completely, as when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> renders its entire <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> unliveable; or may occur gradually over thousands or millions of years, such as when a species gradually loses out in competition for food to better adapted competitors. Extinction may occur a long time after the events that set it in motion, a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Extinction_debt" title="Extinction debt">extinction debt</a>.
</p><p>Assessing the relative importance of genetic factors compared to environmental ones as the causes of extinction has been compared to the debate on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_and_nurture" title="Nature and nurture">nature and nurture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raup_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raup-34">[34]</a></sup> The question of whether more extinctions in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record have been caused by <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> or by competition or by predation or by disease or by catastrophe is a subject of discussion; Mark Newman, the author of <i>Modeling Extinction</i>, argues for a mathematical model that falls in all positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Newman-5">[5]</a></sup> By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a> uses the <a href="/wiki/Extinction_vortex" title="Extinction vortex">extinction vortex</a> model to classify extinctions by cause. When concerns about <a href="/wiki/Human_extinction" title="Human extinction">human extinction</a> have been raised, for example in Sir <a href="/wiki/Martin_Rees" title="Martin Rees">Martin Rees</a>' 2003 book <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Final_Hour" title="Our Final Hour">Our Final Hour</a></i>, those concerns lie with the effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> or <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technological</a> disaster.
</p><p>Currently, environmental groups and some governments are concerned with the extinction of species caused by humanity, and they try to prevent further extinctions through a variety of <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation</a> programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC-9">[9]</a></sup> Humans can cause extinction of a species through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overharvesting" title="Overharvesting">overharvesting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, introduction of <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> (such as new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a> and food <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitors" title="Competitors">competitors</a>), overhunting, and other influences. Explosive, unsustainable human <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">increasing per capita consumption</a> are essential drivers of the extinction crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos2017_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos2017-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN), 784 extinctions have been recorded since the year 1500, the arbitrary date selected to define "recent" extinctions, up to the year 2004; with many more likely to have gone unnoticed. Several species have also been listed as extinct since 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNno_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNno-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics_and_demographic_phenomena">Genetics and demographic phenomena</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Genetics and demographic phenomena">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extinction_vortex" title="Extinction vortex">Extinction vortex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_erosion" title="Genetic erosion">Genetic erosion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mutational_meltdown" title="Mutational meltdown">Mutational meltdown</a></div>
<p>If <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> increasing population <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> is slower than environmental degradation plus the accumulation of slightly deleterious <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a>, then a population will go extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Smaller populations have fewer beneficial mutations entering the population each generation, slowing adaptation. It is also easier for slightly deleterious mutations to <a href="/wiki/Fixation_(population_genetics)" title="Fixation (population genetics)">fix</a> in small populations; the resulting positive feedback loop between small population size and low fitness can cause <a href="/wiki/Mutational_meltdown" title="Mutational meltdown">mutational meltdown</a>.
</p><p>Limited geographic range is the most important determinant of <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> extinction at background rates but becomes increasingly irrelevant as <a href="#Mass_extinctions">mass extinction</a> arises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Limited geographic range is a cause both of small population size and of greater vulnerability to local environmental catastrophes.
</p><p>Extinction rates can be affected not just by population size, but by any factor that affects <a href="/wiki/Evolvability" title="Evolvability">evolvability</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Balancing_selection" title="Balancing selection">balancing selection</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptic_genetic_variation" title="Cryptic genetic variation">cryptic genetic variation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_plasticity" title="Phenotypic plasticity">phenotypic plasticity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robustness_(evolution)" title="Robustness (evolution)">robustness</a>. A diverse or deep <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a> gives a population a higher chance in the short term of surviving an adverse change in conditions. Effects that cause or reward a loss in <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> can increase the chances of extinction of a species. <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">Population bottlenecks</a> can dramatically reduce genetic diversity by severely limiting the number of reproducing individuals and make <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">inbreeding</a> more frequent.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_pollution">Genetic pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Genetic pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">Genetic pollution</a></div>
<p>Extinction sometimes results for species evolved to specific ecologies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> that are subjected to <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">genetic pollution</a>—i.e., uncontrolled <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">introgression</a> and genetic swamping that lead to homogenization or <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">out-competition</a> from the introduced (<a href="/wiki/Heterosis" title="Heterosis">or hybrid</a>) species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Endemic populations can face such extinctions when new populations are imported or <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selectively bred</a> by people, or when habitat modification brings previously isolated species into contact. Extinction is likeliest for <a href="/wiki/Rare_species" title="Rare species">rare species</a> coming into contact with more abundant ones;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RareEucalypts_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-RareEucalypts-44">[44]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interbreeding" title="Interbreeding">interbreeding</a> can swamp the rarer gene pool and create hybrids, depleting the purebred gene pool (for example, the endangered <a href="/wiki/Wild_water_buffalo" title="Wild water buffalo">wild water buffalo</a> is most threatened with extinction by genetic pollution from <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">the abundant domestic water buffalo</a>). Such extinctions are not always apparent from <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> (non-genetic) observations. Some degree of <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> is a normal evolutionary process; nevertheless, hybridization (with or without introgression) threatens rare species' existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gene pool of a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> is the variety of genetic information in its living members. A large gene pool (extensive <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>) is associated with robust populations that can survive bouts of intense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selection_(biology)" title="Selection (biology)">selection</a>. Meanwhile, low genetic diversity (see <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">inbreeding</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_bottlenecks" title="Population bottlenecks">population bottlenecks</a>) reduces the range of adaptions possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Replacing native with alien genes narrows genetic diversity within the original population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RareEucalypts_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-RareEucalypts-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> thereby increasing the chance of extinction.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1065" data-file-width="2034" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg/220px-DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg/330px-DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg/440px-DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_santa_fe_scorched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scorched land resulting from <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> agriculture</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_degradation">Habitat degradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Habitat degradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">Habitat destruction</a></div>
<p>Habitat degradation is currently the main anthropogenic cause of species extinctions. The main cause of habitat degradation worldwide is agriculture, with <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>, logging, mining, and some fishing practices close behind. The degradation of a species' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a> may alter the <a href="/wiki/Fitness_landscape" title="Fitness landscape">fitness landscape</a> to such an extent that the species is no longer able to survive and becomes extinct. This may occur by direct effects, such as the environment becoming <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxic</a>, or indirectly, by limiting a species' ability to compete effectively for diminished resources or against new competitor species.
</p><p>Habitat degradation through toxicity can kill off a species very rapidly, by killing all living members through <a href="/wiki/Contamination" title="Contamination">contamination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilizing</a> them. It can also occur over longer periods at lower toxicity levels by affecting life span, reproductive capacity, or competitiveness.
</p><p>Habitat degradation can also take the form of a physical destruction of niche habitats. The widespread destruction of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a> and replacement with open pastureland is widely cited as an example of this;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-14">[14]</a></sup> elimination of the dense forest eliminated the infrastructure needed by many species to survive. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">fern</a> that depends on dense shade for protection from direct sunlight can no longer survive without forest to shelter it. Another example is the destruction of ocean floors by <a href="/wiki/Bottom_trawling" title="Bottom trawling">bottom trawling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diminished resources or introduction of new competitor species also often accompany habitat degradation. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> has allowed some species to expand their range, bringing unwelcome<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> competition to other species that previously occupied that area. Sometimes these new competitors are predators and directly affect prey species, while at other times they may merely outcompete vulnerable species for limited resources. Vital resources including <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and food can also be limited during habitat degradation, leading to extinction.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2385" data-file-width="3576" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/220px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/330px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Bufo_periglenes2.jpg/440px-Bufo_periglenes2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bufo_periglenes2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Golden_toad" title="Golden toad">golden toad</a> was last seen on May 15, 1989. <a href="/wiki/Decline_in_amphibian_populations" title="Decline in amphibian populations">Decline in amphibian populations</a> is ongoing worldwide.</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Predation.2C_competition.2C_and_disease"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Predation,_competition,_and_disease">Predation, competition, and disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Predation, competition, and disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Island_restoration" title="Island restoration">Island restoration</a></div>
<p>In the natural course of events, species become extinct for a number of reasons, including but not limited to: extinction of a necessary host, prey or pollinator, inter-species competition, inability to deal with evolving diseases and changing environmental conditions (particularly sudden changes) which can act to introduce novel predators, or to remove prey. Recently in geological time, humans have become an additional cause of extinction (some people would say premature extinction<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) of some species, either as a new mega-predator or by <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">transporting</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> from one part of the world to another. Such introductions have been occurring for thousands of years, sometimes intentionally (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> released by sailors on islands as a future source of food) and sometimes accidentally (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a> escaping from boats). In most cases, the introductions are unsuccessful, but when an <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive alien species</a> does become established, the consequences can be catastrophic. Invasive alien species can affect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_(ecology)" title="Endemic (ecology)">native</a> species directly by eating them, competing with them, and introducing <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> that sicken or kill them; or indirectly by destroying or degrading their habitat. Human populations may themselves act as invasive predators. According to the "overkill hypothesis", the swift extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> in areas such as Australia (40,000 years before present), <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> (12,000 years before present), <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> (AD 300–1000), and New Zealand (AD 1300–1500), resulted from the sudden introduction of human beings to environments full of animals that had never seen them before and were therefore completely unadapted to their predation techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coextinction">Coextinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Coextinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coextinction" title="Coextinction">Coextinction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1101" data-file-width="1375" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/220px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/330px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg/440px-Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_Haasts_eagle_attacking_New_Zealand_moa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The large <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a> from New Zealand</div></div></div>
<p>Coextinction refers to the loss of a species due to the extinction of another; for example, the extinction of <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasitic</a> insects following the loss of their hosts. Coextinction can also occur when a species loses its <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinator</a>, or to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predators</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> who lose their prey. "Species coextinction is a manifestation of one of the interconnectednesses of organisms in complex ecosystems ... While coextinction may not be the most important cause of species extinctions, it is certainly an insidious one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koh_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koh-51">[51]</a></sup> Coextinction is especially common when a <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">keystone species</a> goes extinct. Models suggest that coextinction is the most common form of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a>. There may be a cascade of coextinction across the <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic levels</a>. Such effects are most severe in <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistic</a> and parasitic relationships. An example of coextinction is the <a href="/wiki/Haast%27s_eagle" title="Haast's eagle">Haast's eagle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">moa</a>: the Haast's eagle was a predator that became extinct because its food source became extinct. The moa were several species of flightless birds that were a food source for the Haast's eagle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinction_risk_from_global_warming" title="Extinction risk from global warming">Extinction risk from global warming</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effect_of_climate_change_on_plant_biodiversity" title="Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity">Effect of climate change on plant biodiversity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_terrestrial_animals" title="Effects of climate change on terrestrial animals">Effects of climate change on terrestrial animals</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_marine_mammals" title="Effects of climate change on marine mammals">Effects of climate change on marine mammals</a></div>
<p>Extinction as a result of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> has been confirmed by fossil studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-53">[53]</a></sup> Particularly, the extinction of amphibians during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse">Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse</a>, 305 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-53">[53]</a></sup> A 2003 review across 14 biodiversity research centers predicted that, because of climate change, 15–37% of land species would be "committed to extinction" by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhattacharya_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhattacharya-55">[55]</a></sup> The ecologically rich areas that would potentially suffer the heaviest losses include the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Floristic_Region" title="Cape Floristic Region">Cape Floristic Region</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin" title="Caribbean Basin">Caribbean Basin</a>. These areas might see a doubling of present carbon dioxide levels and rising temperatures that could eliminate 56,000 plant and 3,700 animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Climate change has also been found to be a factor in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_selection_and_male_investment">Sexual selection and male investment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sexual selection and male investment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies of fossils following species from the time they evolved to their extinction show that species with high <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, especially characteristics in males that are used to compete for mating, are at a higher risk of extinction and die out faster than less sexually dimorphic species, the least sexually dimorphic species surviving for millions of years while the most sexually dimorphic species die out within mere thousands of years. Earlier studies based on counting the number of currently living species in modern taxa have shown a higher number of species in more sexually dimorphic taxa which have been interpreted as higher survival in taxa with more sexual selection, but such studies of modern species only measure indirect effects of extinction and are subject to error sources such as dying and doomed taxa speciating more due to splitting of habitat ranges into more small isolated groups during the habitat retreat of taxa approaching extinction. Possible causes of the higher extinction risk in species with more sexual selection shown by the comprehensive fossil studies that rule out such error sources include expensive sexually selected ornaments having negative effects on the ability to survive <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> removing a diversity of genes that under current ecological conditions are neutral for natural selection but some of which may be important for surviving climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_extinctions">Mass extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mass extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">Extinction event</a></div>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:332px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:252px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:10px; top:30px; width:320px; position:absolute;"> <div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Extinction_intensity.svg" title="File:Extinction intensity.svg"><img alt="Extinction intensity.svg" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/320px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/480px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/640px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_dd6b8fd031a424b3" width="320"/></a><map name="ImageMap_dd6b8fd031a424b3"><area alt="Cambrian" coords="16,0,46,14" href="/wiki/Cambrian" shape="rect" title="Cambrian"/><area alt="Ordovician" coords="47,0,70,14" href="/wiki/Ordovician" shape="rect" title="Ordovician"/><area alt="Silurian" coords="71,0,86,14" href="/wiki/Silurian" shape="rect" title="Silurian"/><area alt="Devonian" coords="87,0,117,14" href="/wiki/Devonian" shape="rect" title="Devonian"/><area alt="Carboniferous" coords="118,0,151,14" href="/wiki/Carboniferous" shape="rect" title="Carboniferous"/><area alt="Permian" coords="151,0,178,14" href="/wiki/Permian" shape="rect" title="Permian"/><area alt="Triassic" coords="178,0,206,14" href="/wiki/Triassic" shape="rect" title="Triassic"/><area alt="Jurassic" coords="207,0,236,14" href="/wiki/Jurassic" shape="rect" title="Jurassic"/><area alt="Cretaceous" coords="237,0,280,14" href="/wiki/Cretaceous" shape="rect" title="Cretaceous"/><area alt="Paleogene" coords="281,0,304,14" href="/wiki/Paleogene" shape="rect" title="Paleogene"/><area alt="Neogene" coords="304,0,316,14" href="/wiki/Neogene" shape="rect" title="Neogene"/></map></div></div>
