<div id="annotation_70x110" style="position:absolute; left:70px; top:110px; line-height:110%;"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><a href="/wiki/Late_Ordovician_mass_extinction" title="Late Ordovician mass extinction">O–S</a></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Extinction_intensity.svg" title="File:Extinction intensity.svg"><img alt="Extinction intensity.svg" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/320px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/480px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Extinction_intensity.svg/640px-Extinction_intensity.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_dd6b8fd031a424b3" width="320"/></a><map name="ImageMap_dd6b8fd031a424b3"><area alt="Cambrian" coords="16,0,46,14" href="/wiki/Cambrian" shape="rect" title="Cambrian"/><area alt="Ordovician" coords="47,0,70,14" href="/wiki/Ordovician" shape="rect" title="Ordovician"/><area alt="Silurian" coords="71,0,86,14" href="/wiki/Silurian" shape="rect" title="Silurian"/><area alt="Devonian" coords="87,0,117,14" href="/wiki/Devonian" shape="rect" title="Devonian"/><area alt="Carboniferous" coords="118,0,151,14" href="/wiki/Carboniferous" shape="rect" title="Carboniferous"/><area alt="Permian" coords="151,0,178,14" href="/wiki/Permian" shape="rect" title="Permian"/><area alt="Triassic" coords="178,0,206,14" href="/wiki/Triassic" shape="rect" title="Triassic"/><area alt="Jurassic" coords="207,0,236,14" href="/wiki/Jurassic" shape="rect" title="Jurassic"/><area alt="Cretaceous" coords="237,0,280,14" href="/wiki/Cretaceous" shape="rect" title="Cretaceous"/><area alt="Paleogene" coords="281,0,304,14" href="/wiki/Paleogene" shape="rect" title="Paleogene"/><area alt="Neogene" coords="304,0,316,14" href="/wiki/Neogene" shape="rect" title="Neogene"/></map></div></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">The blue graph shows the apparent <i>percentage</i> (not the absolute number) of marine <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> becoming extinct during any given time interval. It does not represent all marine species, just those that are readily fossilized. The labels of the traditional "Big Five" extinction events and the more recently recognised <a href="/wiki/Capitanian_mass_extinction_event" title="Capitanian mass extinction event">Capitanian mass extinction event</a> are clickable links; see <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">Extinction event</a> for more details. <i>(<a href="/wiki/File:Extinction_intensity.svg" title="File:Extinction intensity.svg">source and image info</a>)</i></div>
</div></div>
<p>There have been at least five mass extinctions in the history of life on earth, and four in the last 350 million years in which many species have disappeared in a relatively short period of geological time. A massive eruptive event that released large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">tephra</a> particles into the atmosphere is considered to be one likely cause of the "<a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>" about 250 million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-59">[59]</a></sup> which is estimated to have killed 90% of species then existing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> There is also evidence to suggest that this event was preceded by another mass extinction, known as <a href="/wiki/Olson%27s_Extinction" title="Olson's Extinction">Olson's Extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-59">[59]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> (K–Pg) occurred 66 million years ago, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period; it is best known for having wiped out non-avian <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>, among many other species.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_extinctions">Modern extinctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Modern extinctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Defaunation" title="Defaunation">Defaunation</a></div>
<p>According to a 1998 survey of 400 biologists conducted by <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>'s <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, nearly 70% believed that the Earth is currently in the early stages of a human-caused mass extinction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMNH_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMNH-61">[61]</a></sup> known as the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a>. In that survey, the same proportion of respondents agreed with the prediction that up to 20% of all living populations could become extinct within 30 years (by 2028). A 2014 special edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> declared there is widespread consensus on the issue of human-driven mass species extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>  A 2020 study published in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PNAS" title="PNAS">PNAS</a></i> stated that the contemporary extinction crisis "may be the most serious environmental threat to the persistence of civilization, because it is irreversible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biologist <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> estimated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-14">[14]</a></sup> in 2002 that if current rates of human destruction of the biosphere continue, one-half of all plant and animal species of life on earth will be extinct in 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson2_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson2-64">[64]</a></sup> More significantly, the current rate of global species extinctions is estimated as 100 to 1,000 times "background" rates (the average extinction rates in the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> time scale of planet Earth),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeVos2014_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeVos2014-66">[66]</a></sup> faster than at any other time in human history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> while future rates are likely 10,000 times higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeVos2014_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeVos2014-66">[66]</a></sup> However, some groups are going extinct much faster. Biologists <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Pimm" title="Stuart Pimm">Stuart Pimm</a>, among others, contend that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_population_growth" title="Human population growth">human population growth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">overconsumption</a> are the main drivers of the modern extinction crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos2017_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos2017-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2020, the UN's <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> drafted a plan to mitigate the contemporary extinction crisis by establishing a deadline of 2030 to protect 30% of the earth's land and oceans and reduce pollution by 50%, with the goal of allowing for the restoration of ecosystems by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The 2020 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' <i>Global Biodiversity Outlook</i> report stated that of the 20 biodiversity goals laid out by the Aichi Biodiversity Targets in 2010, only 6 were "partially achieved" by the deadline of 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The report warned that biodiversity will continue to decline if the status quo is not changed, in particular the "currently unsustainable patterns of production and consumption, population growth and technological developments".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In a 2021 report published in the journal <i>Frontiers in Conservation Science</i>, some top scientists asserted that even if the Aichi Biodiversity Targets set for 2020 had been achieved, it would not have resulted in a significant mitigation of biodiversity loss. They added that failure of the global community to reach these targets is hardly surprising given that biodiversity loss is "nowhere close to the top of any country's priorities, trailing far behind other concerns such as employment, healthcare, economic growth, or currency stability."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_scientific_understanding">History of scientific understanding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: History of scientific understanding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png/220px-Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png/330px-Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png/440px-Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tyrannosaurus-rex-Profile-steveoc86.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>, one of the many extinct dinosaur genera. The cause of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> is a subject of much debate amongst researchers.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2038" data-file-width="2661" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg/220px-Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg/330px-Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg/440px-Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuvier_elephant_jaw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Georges Cuvier</a> compared fossil <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> jaws to those of living elephants, concluding that they were distinct from any known living species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>For much of history, the modern understanding of extinction as the end of a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> was incompatible with the prevailing worldview. Prior to the 19th century, much of Western society adhered to the belief that the world was created by God and as such was complete and perfect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-79">[79]</a></sup> This concept reached its heyday in the 1700s with the peak popularity of a theological concept called the <a href="/wiki/Great_chain_of_being" title="Great chain of being">great chain of being</a>, in which all life on earth, from the tiniest microorganism to God, is linked in a continuous chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> The extinction of a species was impossible under this model, as it would create gaps or missing links in the chain and destroy the natural order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> was a firm supporter of the great chain of being and an opponent of extinction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-:02-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup> famously denying the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> on the grounds that nature never allows a race of animals to become extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:42-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>A series of fossils were discovered in the late 17th century that appeared unlike any living species. As a result, the scientific community embarked on a voyage of creative rationalization, seeking to understand what had happened to these species within a framework that did not account for total extinction. In October 1686, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> presented an impression of a <a href="/wiki/Nautilus" title="Nautilus">nautilus</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> that was more than two feet in diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and morphologically distinct from any known living species. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Hooke</a> theorized that this was simply because the species lived in the deep ocean and no one had discovered them yet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> While he contended that it was possible a species could be "lost", he thought this highly unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> Similarly, in 1695, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Molyneux" title="Sir Thomas Molyneux">Sir Thomas Molyneux</a> published an account of enormous antlers found in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> that did not belong to any extant taxa in that area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-84">[84]</a></sup> Molyneux reasoned that they came from the North American <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a> and that the animal had once been common on the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-:22-84">[84]</a></sup> Rather than suggest that this indicated the possibility of species going extinct, he argued that although organisms could become locally extinct, they could never be entirely lost and would continue to exist in some unknown region of the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-:22-84">[84]</a></sup> The antlers were later confirmed to be from the extinct <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Megaloceros" title="Megaloceros">Megaloceros</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup> Hooke and Molyneux's line of thinking was difficult to disprove. When parts of the world had not been thoroughly examined and charted, scientists could not rule out that animals found only in the fossil record were not simply "hiding" in unexplored regions of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Georges_Cuvier" title="Georges Cuvier">Georges Cuvier</a> is credited with establishing the modern conception of extinction in a 1796 lecture to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Institute" title="French Institute">French Institute</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:42-82">[82]</a></sup> though he would spend most of his career trying to convince the wider scientific community of his theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup> Cuvier was a well-regarded geologist, lauded for his ability to reconstruct the anatomy of an unknown species from a few fragments of bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup> His primary evidence for extinction came from mammoth skulls found in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Basin" title="Paris Basin">Paris basin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup> Cuvier recognized them as distinct from any known living species of elephant, and argued that it was highly unlikely such an enormous animal would go undiscovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup> In 1812, Cuvier, along with <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Brongniart" title="Alexandre Brongniart">Alexandre Brongniart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isidore_Geoffroy_Saint-Hilaire" title="Isidore Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire">Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire</a>, mapped the <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a> of the Paris basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> They saw alternating saltwater and freshwater deposits, as well as patterns of the appearance and disappearance of fossils throughout the record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-4"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup> From these patterns, Cuvier inferred historic cycles of catastrophic flooding, extinction, and repopulation of the earth with new species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-5"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cuvier's fossil evidence showed that very different life forms existed in the past than those that exist today, a fact that was accepted by most scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-:02-79">[79]</a></sup> The primary debate focused on whether this turnover caused by extinction was gradual or abrupt in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup> Cuvier understood extinction to be the result of cataclysmic events that wipe out huge numbers of species, as opposed to the gradual decline of a species over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-87">[87]</a></sup> His catastrophic view of the nature of extinction garnered him many opponents in the newly emerging school of <a href="/wiki/Uniformitarianism" title="Uniformitarianism">uniformitarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gradualism" title="Gradualism">gradualist</a> and colleague of Cuvier, saw the fossils of different life forms as evidence of the mutable character of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup> While Lamarck did not deny the possibility of extinction, he believed that it was exceptional and rare and that most of the change in species over time was due to gradual change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-5"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup> Unlike Cuvier, Lamarck was skeptical that catastrophic events of a scale large enough to cause total extinction were possible. In his geological history of the earth titled Hydrogeologie, Lamarck instead argued that the surface of the earth was shaped by gradual erosion and deposition by water, and that species changed over time in response to the changing environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:62_86-6"><a href="#cite_note-:62-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>, a noted geologist and founder of <a href="/wiki/Uniformitarianism" title="Uniformitarianism">uniformitarianism</a>, believed that past processes should be understood using present day processes. Like Lamarck, Lyell acknowledged that extinction could occur, noting the total extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a> and the extirpation of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_horse_in_Britain" title="History of the horse in Britain">indigenous horses</a> to the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:12_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-:12-80">[80]</a></sup> He similarly argued against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a>, believing that any extinction must be a gradual process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:52_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-:52-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-:42-82">[82]</a></sup> Lyell also showed that Cuvier's original interpretation of the Parisian strata was incorrect. Instead of the catastrophic floods inferred by Cuvier, Lyell demonstrated that patterns of saltwater and freshwater <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposits</a>, like those seen in the Paris basin, could be formed by a slow rise and fall of <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_81-6"><a href="#cite_note-:32-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of extinction was integral to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>, with less fit lineages disappearing over time. For Darwin, extinction was a constant side effect of <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">competition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Because of the wide reach of <i>On the Origin of Species</i>, it was widely accepted that extinction occurred gradually and evenly (a concept now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Background_extinction_rate" title="Background extinction rate">background extinction</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-:42-82">[82]</a></sup> It was not until 1982, when <a href="/wiki/David_M._Raup" title="David M. Raup">David Raup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Sepkoski" title="Jack Sepkoski">Jack Sepkoski</a> published their seminal paper on mass extinctions, that Cuvier was vindicated and catastrophic extinction was accepted as an important mechanism. The current understanding of extinction is a synthesis of the cataclysmic extinction events proposed by Cuvier, and the background extinction events proposed by Lyell and Darwin.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_attitudes_and_interests">Human attitudes and interests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Human attitudes and interests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1945" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg/180px-Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg/270px-Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg/360px-Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphyrna_mokarran_fishing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Great_hammerhead" title="Great hammerhead">great hammerhead</a> caught by a sport fisherman. Human exploitation now threatens the survival of this species. <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">Overfishing</a> is the primary driver of shark population declines, which have fallen over 71% since 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Extinction is an important research topic in the field of <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> in general, and has also become an area of concern outside the scientific community. A number of organizations, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WWF_(conservation_organization)" title="WWF (conservation organization)">Worldwide Fund for Nature</a>, have been created with  the goal of preserving species from extinction. <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">Governments</a> have attempted, through enacting laws, to avoid habitat destruction, agricultural over-harvesting, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>. While many human-caused extinctions have been accidental, humans have also engaged in the deliberate destruction of some species, such as dangerous <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, and the total destruction of other problematic species has been suggested. Other species were deliberately driven to extinction, or nearly so, due to poaching or because they were "undesirable", or to push for other human agendas. One example was the near extinction of the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">American bison</a>, which was nearly wiped out by mass hunts sanctioned by the United States government, to force the removal of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>, many of whom relied on the bison for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kotzman_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kotzman-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biologist Bruce Walsh states three reasons for scientific interest in the preservation of species: genetic resources, ecosystem stability, and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>; and today the scientific community "stress[es] the importance" of maintaining biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CREOcare_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-CREOcare-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, commercial and industrial interests often have to contend with the effects of production on plant and animal life. However, some technologies with minimal, or no, proven harmful effects on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> can be devastating to wildlife (for example, <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INCHEM_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-INCHEM-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">Biogeographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> notes that while <a href="/wiki/Big_business" title="Big business">big business</a> may label environmental concerns as "exaggerated", and often cause "devastating damage", some corporations find it in their interest to adopt good conservation practices, and even engage in preservation efforts that surpass those taken by <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiamondCollapse_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiamondCollapse-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Governments sometimes see the loss of native species as a loss to <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drewry_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drewry-98">[98]</a></sup> and can enact laws with severe punishment against the trade in native species in an effort to prevent extinction in the wild. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_preserve" title="Nature preserve">Nature preserves</a> are created by governments as a means to provide continuing habitats to species crowded by human expansion. The 1992 <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a> has resulted in international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Action_Plan" title="Biodiversity Action Plan">Biodiversity Action Plan</a> programmes, which attempt to provide comprehensive guidelines for government biodiversity conservation. Advocacy groups, such as The Wildlands Project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WildlandsProject_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-WildlandsProject-99">[99]</a></sup> and the Alliance for Zero Extinctions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> work to educate the public and pressure governments into action.
</p><p>People who live close to nature can be dependent on the survival of all the species in their environment, leaving them highly exposed to extinction <a href="/wiki/Risk" title="Risk">risks</a>. However, people prioritize day-to-day survival over species conservation; with <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">human overpopulation</a> in tropical <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, there has been enormous pressure on forests due to <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence agriculture</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> agricultural techniques that can reduce endangered species's habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antinatalist" title="Antinatalist">Antinatalist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Benatar" title="David Benatar">David Benatar</a> concludes that any popular concern about non-human species extinction usually arises out of concern about how the loss of a species will impact human wants and needs, that "we shall live in a world impoverished by the loss of one aspect of faunal diversity, that we shall no longer be able to behold or use that species of animal." He notes that typical concerns about possible human extinction, such as the loss of individual members, are not considered in regards to non-human species extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Jason_Hickel" title="Jason Hickel">Jason Hickel</a> speculates that the reason humanity seems largely indifferent to anthropogenic mass species extinction is that we see ourselves as separate from the natural world and the organisms within it. He says that this is due in part to the logic of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>: "that the world is not really alive, and it is certainly not our kin, but rather just stuff to be extracted and discarded – and that includes most of the human beings living here too."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planned_extinction">Planned extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Planned extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">Eradication of infectious diseases</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Completed">Completed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Completed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> virus is now extinct in the wild,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Factsheet_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Factsheet-104">[104]</a></sup> although samples are retained in laboratory settings.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a> virus, which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattle_diseases" title="Cattle diseases">infected domestic cattle</a>, is now extinct in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Normile2008_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Normile2008-105">[105]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed">Proposed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Proposed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">poliovirus</a> is now confined to small parts of the world due to extermination efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polio_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-polio-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Dracunculus_medinensis" title="Dracunculus medinensis">Dracunculus medinensis</a></i>, or Guinea worm, a parasitic worm which causes the disease <a href="/wiki/Dracunculiasis" title="Dracunculiasis">dracunculiasis</a>, is now close to eradication thanks to efforts led by the <a href="/wiki/Carter_Center" title="Carter Center">Carter Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Treponema_pallidum" title="Treponema pallidum">Treponema pallidum pertenue</a></i>, a bacterium which causes the disease <a href="/wiki/Yaws" title="Yaws">yaws</a>, is in the process of being eradicated.
</p><p>Biologist <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Judson" title="Olivia Judson">Olivia Judson</a> has advocated the deliberate extinction of certain disease-carrying <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a> species. In a September 25, 2003 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, she advocated "specicide" of thirty mosquito species by introducing a genetic element that can insert itself into another crucial gene, to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive" title="Recessive">recessive</a> "<a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">knockout genes</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Judson_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Judson-108">[108]</a></sup> She says that the <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> mosquitoes (which spread <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Aedes" title="Aedes">Aedes</a></i> mosquitoes (which spread <a href="/wiki/Dengue_fever" title="Dengue fever">dengue fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elephantiasis_tropica" title="Elephantiasis tropica">elephantiasis</a>, and other diseases) represent only 30 of around 3,500 mosquito species; eradicating these would save at least one million human lives per annum, at a cost of reducing the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culicidae" title="Culicidae">Culicidae</a> by only 1%. She further argues that since species become extinct "all the time" the disappearance of a few more will not destroy the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>: "We're not left with a wasteland every time a species vanishes. Removing one species sometimes causes shifts in the populations of other species—but different need not mean worse." In addition, anti-malarial and <a href="/wiki/Mosquito#Control" title="Mosquito">mosquito control programs</a> offer little realistic hope to the 300 million people in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nation" title="Developing nation">developing nations</a> who will be infected with acute illnesses this year. Although trials are ongoing, she writes that if they fail "we should consider the ultimate swatting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Judson_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Judson-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Biologist <a href="/wiki/E._O._Wilson" title="E. O. Wilson">E. O. Wilson</a> has advocated the eradication of several species of mosquito, including malaria vector <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles_gambiae" title="Anopheles gambiae">Anopheles gambiae</a></i>. Wilson stated, "I'm talking about a very small number of species that have co-evolved with us and are preying on humans, so it would certainly be acceptable to remove them. I believe it's just common sense."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been many campaigns – some successful – to locally eradicate <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">tsetse flies</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Trypanosoma" title="Trypanosoma">trypanosomes</a> in areas, countries, and islands of Africa (including <a href="/wiki/Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="Príncipe">Príncipe</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa-et-al-1916_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-et-al-1916-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-book-review-1916_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-book-review-1916-111">[111]</a></sup> There are currently serious efforts to do away with them all across Africa, and this is generally viewed as beneficial and morally necessary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simarro-et-al-2008_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simarro-et-al-2008-112">[112]</a></sup> although not always.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouyer-et-al-2018_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bouyer-et-al-2018-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloning">Cloning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cloning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/De-extinction" title="De-extinction">De-extinction</a></div>
<p>Some, such as Harvard geneticist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_M._Church" title="George M. Church">George M. Church</a>, believe that ongoing technological advances will let us "bring back to life" an extinct species by <a href="/wiki/Cloning#Cloning_extinct_and_endangered_species" title="Cloning">cloning</a>, using <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> from the remains of that species. Proposed targets for cloning include the <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Thylacine" title="Thylacine">thylacine</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenean_ibex" title="Pyrenean ibex">Pyrenean ibex</a>. For this to succeed, enough individuals would have to be cloned, from the DNA of different individuals (in the case of sexually reproducing organisms) to create a viable population. Though <a href="/wiki/Bioethics" title="Bioethics">bioethical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> objections have been raised,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> the cloning of extinct creatures seems theoretically possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, scientists tried to clone the extinct Pyrenean ibex (<i>C. p. pyrenaica</i>). This attempt failed: of the 285 embryos reconstructed, 54 were transferred to 12 <a href="/wiki/Mountain_goat" title="Mountain goat">mountain goats</a> and mountain goat–domestic <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a> hybrids, but only two survived the initial two months of gestation before they, too, died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In 2009, a second attempt was made to clone the Pyrenean ibex: one clone was born alive, but died seven minutes later, due to physical defects in the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Extinction&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-Ceballos2017-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ceballos2017_35-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ceballos2017_35-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFCeballosEhrlichDirzo2017">Ceballos, Gerardo; Ehrlich, Paul R; Dirzo, Rodolfo (23 May 2017). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5544311" rel="nofollow">"Biological annihilation via the ongoing sixth mass extinction signaled by vertebrate population losses and declines"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America">PNAS</a></i>. <b>114</b> (30): E6089–E6096. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.1704949114" rel="nofollow">10.1073/pnas.1704949114</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5544311" rel="nofollow">5544311</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/28696295" rel="nofollow">28696295</a>. <q>Much less frequently mentioned are, however, the ultimate drivers of those immediate causes of biotic destruction, namely, human overpopulation and continued population growth, and overconsumption, especially by the rich. These drivers, all of which trace to the fiction that perpetual growth can occur on a finite planet, are themselves increasing rapidly.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=PNAS&amp;rft.atitle=Biological+annihilation+via+the+ongoing+sixth+mass+extinction+signaled+by+vertebrate+population+losses+and+declines&amp;rft.volume=114&amp;rft.issue=30&amp;rft.pages=E6089-E6096&amp;rft.date=2017-05-23&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC5544311%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F28696295&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1073%2Fpnas.1704949114&amp;rft.aulast=Ceballos&amp;rft.aufirst=Gerardo&amp;rft.au=Ehrlich%2C+Paul+R&amp;rft.au=Dirzo%2C+Rodolfo&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC5544311&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEhrlich1981">Ehrlich, Anne (1981). <i>Extinction: The Causes and Consequences of the Disappearance of Species</i>. Random House, New York. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-394-51312-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-394-51312-6"><bdi>978-0-394-51312-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Extinction%3A+The+Causes+and+Consequences+of+the+Disappearance+of+Species&amp;rft.pub=Random+House%2C+New+York&amp;rft.date=1981&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-394-51312-6&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrlich&amp;rft.aufirst=Anne&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBenatar2008">Benatar, David (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/betternevertohav0000bena/page/197" rel="nofollow"><i>Better Never to Have Been: The Harm of Coming into Existence</i></a>. Oxford University Press. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/betternevertohav0000bena/page/197" rel="nofollow">197</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-954926-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-954926-9"><bdi>978-0-19-954926-9</bdi></a>. <q>It is noteworthy that human concern about human extinction takes a different form from human concern (where there is any) about the extinction of non-human species. Most humans who are concerned about the extinction of non-human species are not concerned about the individual animals whose lives are cut short in the passage to extinction, even though that is one of the best reasons to be concerned about extinction (at least in its killing form). The popular concern about animal extinction is usually concern for humans—that we shall live in a world impoverished by the loss of one aspect of faunal diversity, that we shall no longer be able to behold or use that species of animal. In other words, none of the typical concerns about human extinction are applied to non-human species extinction.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Better+Never+to+Have+Been%3A+The+Harm+of+Coming+into+Existence&amp;rft.pages=197&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-954926-9&amp;rft.aulast=Benatar&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fbetternevertohav0000bena%2Fpage%2F197&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHickel2021"><a href="/wiki/Jason_Hickel" title="Jason Hickel">Hickel, Jason</a> (2021). <i>Less is More: How Degrowth Will Save the World</i>. Windmill Books. p. 80. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1786091215" title="Special:BookSources/978-1786091215"><bdi>978-1786091215</bdi></a>. <q>It's no wonder that we react so nonchalantly to the ever-mounting statistics about the crisis of mass extinction. We have a habit of taking this information with surprising calm. We don't weep. We don't get worked up. Why? Because we see humans as fundamentally separate from the rest of the living community. Those species are out there, in the environment. They aren't in here; they aren't part of us. It is not surprising that we behave this way. After all, this is the core principle of capitalism: that the world is not really alive, and it is certainly not our kin, but rather just stuff to be extracted and discarded – and that includes most of the human beings living here too. From its very first principles, capitalism has set itself at war against life itself.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Less+is+More%3A+How+Degrowth+Will+Save+the+World&amp;rft.pages=80&amp;rft.pub=Windmill+Books&amp;rft.date=2021&amp;rft.isbn=978-1786091215&amp;rft.aulast=Hickel&amp;rft.aufirst=Jason&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-WHO_Factsheet-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-WHO_Factsheet_104-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070921235036/http://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/smallpox/en/" rel="nofollow">"Smallpox"</a>. <i>WHO Factsheet</i>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="https://www.who.int/mediacentre/factsheets/smallpox/en/" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 21 September 2007.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=WHO+Factsheet&amp;rft.atitle=Smallpox&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.who.int%2Fmediacentre%2Ffactsheets%2Fsmallpox%2Fen%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFNicholas_wade2008">Nicholas wade (19 November 2008). <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2008/11/20/science/20mammoth.html?_r=1" rel="nofollow">"Regenerating a Mammoth for $10 Million"</a>. <i>The New York Times</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">17 May</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=Regenerating+a+Mammoth+for+%2410+Million&amp;rft.date=2008-11-19&amp;rft.au=Nicholas+wade&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2008%2F11%2F20%2Fscience%2F20mammoth.html%3F_r%3D1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span> <i>The cell could be converted into an embryo and brought to term by an elephant, a project he estimated would cost some $10 million. "This is something that could work, though it will be tedious and expensive," </i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFSteve_Connor2009">Steve Connor (2 February 2009). <a class="external text" href="https://www.independent.co.uk/news/science/cloned-goat-dies-after-attempt-to-bring-species-back-from-extinction-1522974.html" rel="nofollow">"Cloned goat dies after attempt to bring species back from extinction"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i>. London<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">17 May</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Independent&amp;rft.atitle=Cloned+goat+dies+after+attempt+to+bring+species+back+from+extinction&amp;rft.date=2009-02-02&amp;rft.au=Steve+Connor&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.independent.co.uk%2Fnews%2Fscience%2Fcloned-goat-dies-after-attempt-to-bring-species-back-from-extinction-1522974.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AExtinction"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFGrayDobson2009">Gray, Richard; Dobson, Roger (31 January 2009). <span class="cs1-lock-subscription" title="Paid subscription required"><a class="external text" href="https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/science/science-news/4409958/Extinct-ibex-is-resurrected-by-cloning.html" rel="nofollow">"Extinct ibex is resurrected by cloning"</a></span>. <i>Telegraph</i>.
